# The Devil Hulk  —  *The Devil Hulk*

![[_attachments/the_devil_hulk.png]]

# The Devil Hulk — UATU INTELLIGENCE DOSSIER

*Full-existence intelligence briefing. Independent, evidence-based assessment — the benevolent inverse of total surveillance.*


## Executive Brief (BLUF)

# EXECUTIVE BRIEF (BLUF)

*Classification: Eyes-only synthesis. Read this page if you read nothing else in the dossier. Everything that follows in subsequent sections is annotation on what is stated here.*

---

## BOTTOM LINE UP FRONT

**The subject is a child's protective fantasy that survived to adulthood with its faculties intact, was granted a body capable of executing on its promises, and has spent the subjective equivalent of forty years in a basement waiting to be useful.** He is not a monster, not a villain, not a berserker, not a split-off rage state. He is an *introjected idealized protector* — the imaginary adult a battered boy in Ohio built out of the only raw material available to him (his father's affect, methods, size, and certainty) and then *re-pointed back at the source*. The Devil Hulk is Brian Banner's manner in the service of Brian Banner's victim. That is the entire person, distilled. Every behavior in the record — the patience, the smile, the courtesy before execution, the parental cadence toward Bruce, the methodical investigative work, the willingness to be tortured, the refusal of integration — is downstream of that single structural fact.

He should be understood as the *most adaptive* of the Hulk personae, not the worst. Savage Hulk is a panicked toddler. Joe Fixit is an adolescent delinquent. Professor Hulk is a brittle compromise that fails under sufficient load. Banner is the wounded host. The Devil Hulk is the only configuration in the system that holds executive function under sustained external threat without dysregulating. The system's earlier framing of him as the "worst-case" alter (caged behind iron bars in the Apartment Building inner-world, c. *Incredible Hulk* Vol. 2 #13) reflects the host's *fear* of him, not his actual operational character. The *Immortal Hulk* arc (2018–2021) is, in essence, the long record of the system discovering — under conditions of overwhelming threat (Shadow Base, Fortean, the Leader, the One Below All) — that the thing it had been most afraid of was the thing it most needed.

---

## THE THROUGH-LINE

There is one sentence that runs the length of the subject's existence. He says a version of it in 2000, in chains, behind bars, to a hypnotized Bruce: *I have waited for you. I love you. I will kill the ones who hurt you, beginning with your father.* He acts out a version of it in 2019, free, at night, leading a community of gamma-mutates against a paramilitary apparatus. He completes a version of it in the Below-Place, in front of Bruce, with Brian Banner's soul in his hands, repeatedly, ritually, until the debt is paid and the ritual itself becomes the point. **The promise is the person.** Strip away the morphology and the gamma physics and what remains is a vow made by a child to himself in a room he could not leave, kept across decades, kept literally, kept past the death of its original object, kept past the death of the host, kept into hell.

This is the through-line: **a protective vow that has organized itself into a personality.** Everything operational follows. He is patient because the vow has no expiration. He is calm because the vow's fulfillment is, in his ontology, certain. He is courteous to enemies because they are already, in the calculus of the vow, dead — courtesy costs him nothing. He is parental toward Bruce because the vow is filial in reverse: the child invented the father who should have existed, and now that invented father stands over the adult survivor, hand on shoulder, watching the room. He is hierarchical with the gamma community because they are *Bruce's*, and the vow extends to what Bruce loves. He resists integration because integration ends the vow, and a vow is the only thing he is.

---

## THE CORE WOUND AND ITS MAINTENANCE

**The wound is not the gamma bomb. The wound is the house on Elm Street in Dayton, Ohio, sometime between Bruce's third and ninth years.** A man named Brian Banner — alcoholic, intellectually frustrated, convinced his son was a "mutant" — beat his wife in front of the boy, beat the boy, and eventually killed the wife. The gamma accident two decades later did not cause Bruce's dissociation; it *gave the dissociation somewhere to go*. The personae were already constructed. The bomb merely supplied a body large enough to house the ones the child had built for impossible jobs.

The Devil Hulk is the alter built for the most impossible job of all: **to be bigger than Brian Banner.** Not stronger in the simple physical sense — Bruce as Savage Hulk is already stronger than any human father could be — but *bigger as a presence*. Brian's terror-radius was psychological: his stillness before violence, his unpredictability, his certainty, his smile. A child who is hit by an angry man learns to fear anger; a child who is hit by a calm, smiling, articulate man learns to fear calm, smiles, and articulation. The Devil Hulk's signature traits — the deliberate cadence, the unbluffing courtesy, the smile that means a decision has already been made — are the *exact* sensory profile of Brian Banner at his most frightening, captured by the boy in the act of being terrorized, stored in the basement of the psyche, and re-pointed.

**Maintenance.** The wound is maintained by three structural facts, all of which the Devil Hulk *requires* in order to exist:

1. **The system must remain dissociative.** Full integration would dissolve him. He knows this. His resistance to Professor-Hulk-style mergers, to Doc Samson's therapy, to any framework that proposes one Banner, is not malice — it is self-preservation. He is a vow, and an integrated Bruce no longer needs the vow to be a separate person.

2. **A target must always exist.** Brian Banner was the original. When Brian died, the vow did not. The Devil Hulk's transition from caged threat (2000) to night-shift executive (2018) is, in part, the discovery that the world is full of Brian-substitutes: Fortean, the Leader, the men in the gas station, the Hulkbusters, the abusers of the gamma-touched. He sublimates the unfinished business of the original target onto a structurally identical class of targets. When the Below-Place restored access to Brian himself, the violence became liturgical — not because he had not done it before, but because he had *only ever* been doing it before, on substitutes.

3. **Bruce must remain a child, in his perception.** The Devil Hulk addresses Bruce as *my boy*. He does not address him as a peer, an adult, a colleague, or a host. The parental stance is not a pose; it is the only stance from which the vow is coherent. If Bruce is an adult capable of protecting himself, the vow is obsolete. Therefore Bruce, in the Devil Hulk's internal map, is permanently the boy in the basement, and the Devil Hulk is permanently the adult who arrived in time. This is the *cost* of his protection — Bruce is not permitted, by this alter, to grow up. The infantilization is the price of the bodyguard.

The wound is therefore *load-bearing*. It is not a scar that has healed wrong; it is a foundation. Removing it removes him. He knows this. The entire endgame of the *Immortal Hulk* arc — in which he eventually accepts surrender of his executive position — is the moment in which he chooses, freely, to let the foundation be examined. It is the single most significant act of his existence, because it is the only act that contradicts the vow's logic of permanence.

---

## OPERATIONAL SUMMARY

For purposes of any agency, ally, adversary, or clinician interacting with the subject, the following operational characteristics are determinative.

**Activation.** Circadian. He emerges at sundown and recedes at sunrise. This is not a metaphor and not a stressor-trigger — it is a biological binding, closer to a sleep-state intrusion than a fight-or-flight response. Daylight engagement is engagement with Banner, Savage, or whoever holds the day shift; after dusk, assume the Devil Hulk is in the body. The single most actionable piece of intelligence in this entire dossier is the clock.

**Tempo.** Slow. He is the only entity in the Hulk system that operates on a horizon longer than minutes. He plans across weeks and arcs. He will accept short-term losses — capture, mutilation, humiliation, the appearance of defeat — as positions in a longer game. Adversaries who read short-term capitulation as victory have, in every recorded instance, been wrong.

**Escalation curve (inverted).** Conventional adversaries escalate by losing composure. He escalates by *gaining* it. Stress narrows his target set and slows his cadence. The calmer he becomes, the closer the violence is. The smile is the terminal indicator. When he smiles and lowers his voice, the decision has been made and the outcome is no longer negotiable.

**Threat aperture.** Narrow and rule-bound. He does not threaten passersby, does not torture for sport, does not kill the unaligned. His violence is *adjudicated* — every target has been judged, and the judgment is articulable. This is not restraint in the moral sense; it is *discipline* in the operational sense. He is not safer for being disciplined; he is more dangerous, because the discipline makes his violence predictable in target but unpredictable in timing.

**Loyalty structure (feudal).** Liege: Bruce. Retainers: Betty (Red Harpy), Rick Jones, Jackie McGee, the gamma-mutate community, Joe Fixit and Savage Hulk as subordinate alters. Enemies: Brian Banner (terminal, accomplished), Fortean and the Hulkbusters (operational), the Leader (existential), anyone who has touched what is his. Neutrals are tolerated until reclassified. Reclassification is not appealable.

**Pressure points.** Four, in descending order of usefulness:
- *Bruce's autonomy framed as exile of the Devil Hulk.* He fears irrelevance more than death. Any therapeutic or strategic frame that proposes Bruce can protect himself without him is destabilizing.
- *Honesty violations from within the system.* He treats internal deception as sacrilege. An alter or a Bruce who lies to him fractures the loyalty structure from the top.
- *Denial of legitimate target.* When no proper target exists, he manufactures one from available material. Containment requires either a target or a substitute occupation; absent both, his target-selection apparatus drifts toward whoever is nearest and most marginally justifiable.
- *The mirror.* The single accusation he cannot absorb is *you are Brian Banner.* His entire project is the wager that *use purifies origin* — that doing his father's affect in his father's enemy's service redeems the inheritance. Force him to look at the resemblance directly and the defense structure trembles, briefly. This is the only known method of producing visible affect-dysregulation in him, and it should be deployed only by operators prepared for what follows.

**Containment.** Caging fails (Hulkbusters arc, definitively). Killing fails (resurrection, nightly, via the Green Door). Suppression fails (he is patient enough to outwait any suppression regime the system can sustain). The only documented containment that has held is *recruitment* — assigning him work that uses his actual capacities (leadership of the gamma community, investigation, judgment) within a frame he accepts. He is governable. He is not suppressible.

**Engagement protocol for benevolent parties.** Treat him as an adult. Do not patronize. Do not lie. Address him by what he is (a protector) rather than what he appears to be (a threat). Do not propose his elimination as the price of peace; propose his employment. Acknowledge the vow without ridiculing it. He will, in return, extend a courtesy that is genuine within its limits — limits which are the entire moral universe he has constructed and which he will not exceed in either direction.

---

## ONE-PARAGRAPH SYNTHESIS

The Devil Hulk is the answer a frightened, intelligent, abandoned child gave to a question no child should have to answer: *who will protect me from the man in this house?* The answer he gave was *I will build one.* He built one out of the man in the house — because that was the only model of adult power he had seen — and he kept the construction in a room in his mind where the man could not reach it. The construction waited. It waited through the rest of the childhood, through the adolescence, through Los Alamos, through the bomb, through every other personality the gamma manufactured to handle the surface of Bruce's life. When Bruce was finally killed — shot in the head in a diner — and the system was forced to reorganize from the bottom up, the construction in the basement stood up, walked up the stairs, opened the door, and went to work. He has been working since. He will keep working until the work is done or until the host he was built to protect finally tells him, freely and as an adult, that the work is over. Everything else in this file is detail.


## Chronology I — Origin & Childhood

# CHRONOLOGY I — ORIGIN & CHILDHOOD

*Subject: THE DEVIL HULK. File segment: pre-emergence substrate, ages 0 through approximately 10. Note on method: the Devil Hulk has no separate biological birth; he is an alter constituted inside Robert Bruce Banner's psyche during the period of severe paternal abuse in Banner's childhood. This section therefore reconstructs the developmental ground in which the alter was assembled, and locates — as precisely as the canon allows — the specific events, rooms, and exchanges that would later be metabolized into his voice, his cadence, and his standing orders. Primary canonical sources: Bill Mantlo & Mike Mignola,* Incredible Hulk *#312 ("Monster," 1985); Peter David,* Incredible Hulk *Vol. 1 #377 ("Honey, I Shrunk the Hulk"), #418–420 (Banner/Betty wedding memory inserts), and the* Hulk: The End *coda (2002); Paul Jenkins,* Incredible Hulk *Vol. 2 #12–15 (2000), particularly #13 ("The Boss") and #15 ("Shadows and Light"); Greg Pak,* World War Hulk: Gamma Corps *#1–4 (2009) for Brian Banner's medical/criminal sheet; Al Ewing,* Immortal Hulk *#3 ("Point of View"), #6 ("Patient Zero"), #25 ("There's Always A Choice"), and #34–35 (the One Below All / Brian Banner inner-room sequences, 2018–2020).*

---

## 1. BIRTH AND ANTECEDENTS

**Born:** Robert Bruce Banner, in Dayton, Ohio. Father: Dr. Brian David Banner, atomic physicist, alcoholic, employed at the time at a federally funded nuclear research facility. Mother: Rebecca Walters Banner. Single child. The pregnancy was unwanted by the father — a fact Brian states aloud in flashback (*Incredible Hulk* #312) and which Rebecca relays to the infant in the cadence of an apology rather than a statement. The hospital record is unremarkable; the home that received the newborn was not.

**Pre-natal substrate (per *Incredible Hulk* #312 and #377 retrospectives).** Brian Banner's own father, Bruce's paternal grandfather, was physically abusive. Brian as a child suffered beatings, was told he was defective, and went on to develop two convictions that organized his adulthood: (a) that genetic damage propagated, and (b) that his own work on atomic energy had irradiated his gametes and would produce a "monster" child. When Rebecca conceived, Brian's fear collapsed into certainty. Before Bruce was born his father had already named him a contaminant. The Devil Hulk's later self-description — *waiting in the basement since before you were old enough to know I was there* (paraphrased from IH Vol. 2 #13) — has, as its earliest possible referent, this pre-natal verdict. The room was prepared before the child arrived.

**The first months.** Rebecca Banner is recorded in flashback as warm, attentive, anxiously affectionate (IH #377; IH Vol. 2 #12, the diner-vision flashback). She is the only adult voice in the Banner household that addresses Bruce as a person rather than as a threat. Brian's response to the infant is documented in IH #312: he watches the newborn for "signs," interprets ordinary developmental milestones as evidence of mutation, and refuses to hold him. The household, then, was bifurcated from week one: a mother who attached and a father who surveilled. This is the original split — and the structural template for the later persona-system, in which one identity wraps the host in care and another stands at the door watching for threats.

---

## 2. FAMILY OF ORIGIN — STRUCTURAL MAP

**Brian David Banner (father).** Physicist. Alcoholic. Diagnosable, in retrospect, with at least paranoid personality features and probable alcohol use disorder, with episodes consistent with paranoid delusion under intoxication. Per IH #312 and the Pak *Gamma Corps* sheet: arrested for the murder of Rebecca Banner, institutionalized at a state psychiatric facility, released, later killed by Bruce in a graveside altercation (the headstone incident, IH #312). Operationally relevant qualities preserved by the Devil Hulk alter:
- *Calm, controlled cruelty.* Brian rarely shouted. His abuse was preceded by quiet — the "before" silence is what Bruce learned to monitor. The Devil Hulk's signature affective register — measured, low-volume, courteous before violence — is a direct phenotypic echo.
- *Verbal scaffolding around violence.* Brian explained what he was about to do, and why, in complete sentences, before doing it (IH #312, kitchen scene). The Devil Hulk monologues for the same reason and in roughly the same cadence (IH Vol. 2 #13; *Immortal Hulk* #6, the gas-station verdict).
- *Surveillance posture.* Brian *watched* Bruce — across rooms, across meals, for hours — looking for the "monster." The Devil Hulk's defining posture is watching: hunched forward, head tilted, grin fixed. He inherited the eyes.
- *Categorical thinking.* Brian sorted humans into the clean and the contaminated. The Devil Hulk sorts them into the protected and the targeted. The taxonomy is the same machine, repointed.

**Rebecca Walters Banner (mother).** Homemaker. The canonical record (IH #312; *Immortal Hulk* #34, the inner-room sequence) gives her as soft-spoken, frightened, devoted to her son, and unwilling or unable to leave Brian. She attempted to flee with Bruce on at least one occasion (the bus-station scene, IH #312) and was caught by Brian in the parking lot, where he killed her by slamming her head against the pavement — in front of Bruce, who was approximately six years old. This is the foundational event. Operationally relevant qualities transmitted into the alter:
- *The promise of protection that could not be kept.* Rebecca told Bruce she would get him away. She did not. The Devil Hulk's organizing vow — *I will protect you* — is, in part, Rebecca's promise picked up off the pavement and re-issued by a figure with the physical capacity to honor it.
- *The fact that softness was punished.* The Devil Hulk is not soft. Whatever the alter's love for Bruce is, it does not look like Rebecca's love. He learned which mode of caring survived.

**Extended family.** Susan Banner Drake, Brian's sister, took Bruce in after Brian's institutionalization (IH #312). She appears in canon as kind, competent, and constrained — she could not undo what had been done, but she provided the first prolonged interval of physical safety Bruce had ever experienced. Cousin Jennifer Walters (later She-Hulk), maternally related, became Bruce's only close peer-relative; the relationship is warm in adult canon and traceable to childhood summers (per *Sensational She-Hulk* flashbacks and *Immortal Hulk* #7 dialogue). Importantly: no extended-family figure intervened during the abusive period. The system has no record of an adult who arrived in time. The Devil Hulk's eventual self-conception as the *only* reliable protector is, in part, an accurate reading of the historical evidence.

---

## 3. CHILDHOOD ENVIRONMENT — THE HOUSE

**The Banner home, Dayton, Ohio.** Two-story, suburban, period-appropriate (late 1950s/early 1960s construction; Marvel's sliding timescale keeps the specifics floating). The canonical interior, as rendered repeatedly across IH #312, *Incredible Hulk* #420, *Immortal Hulk* #34, and the *Hulk: The End* memory inserts, includes the following recurring elements:

- **The kitchen.** Site of multiple beatings (IH #312). Linoleum floor, formica table, overhead fluorescent. Bruce's earliest preserved memory in canon (*Immortal Hulk* #3) is of being struck across this table.
- **The basement.** The most behaviorally significant room in the entire dossier. In IH #312 Brian uses the basement as a locking-in punishment — Bruce is sent down, the door is shut, the light is left off or left on at Brian's discretion. The basement is where Bruce learned to wait quietly, to listen for footsteps above, and to construct, in the dark, the *adult who would come down the stairs on his side*. When the Devil Hulk first appears to Doc Samson and Bruce in IH Vol. 2 #13, he is found *in a basement room, behind iron bars*. The internal architecture of Bruce's mind reproduces the Dayton basement with forensic fidelity. The alter lives in the room where he was made.
- **The bedroom.** Small, narrow bed, door without a lock from the inside. Bruce reports (IH #420 wedding-night dialogue with Betty) that he learned to sleep on his side facing the door. This is the somatic seed of the Devil Hulk's circadian binding to night and his watchful, door-facing posture.
- **Brian's study.** Off-limits. Source of the "monster" rhetoric — Brian wrote and re-wrote his theories of genetic contamination at the desk, and Bruce overheard him reading them aloud, drunk, on multiple occasions (IH #312 audio-flashback panels).

**Sensory environment.** Alcohol — Brian's breath, the smell of bourbon on furniture. The sound of glass on wood (a tumbler set down hard is a documented Banner-system trigger; see Devil Hulk's recoil at the same sound in *Immortal Hulk* #6 diner scene, which Ewing stages deliberately). Long silences punctuated by abrupt motion. Television left on as cover for argument. Bruce's adult sleep-pattern dysregulation, his startle response to overhead light changes, and the Devil Hulk's preference for low-lit, enclosed, acoustically dead spaces (diners after close, holding cells, basements) all map to this sensory baseline.

**Caloric and care baseline.** Underfed in periods when Brian was actively drinking; adequately fed when Rebecca could intervene. Medical care minimal — Rebecca treated injuries at home to avoid hospital questions (IH #312). The adult Banner's well-documented aversion to seeking medical attention for himself, and the Devil Hulk's willingness to absorb extraordinary somatic punishment without seeking relief (the Hulkbuster sequences, *Immortal Hulk* #10–11), trace cleanly to this conditioning: pain is endured silently because reporting it produces worse outcomes.

---

## 4. FORMATIVE TRAUMAS — THE EVENT-LIST

In chronological order to the best canonical reconstruction:

**(a) Pre-verbal: the unwanted infant.** Brian's refusal to hold Bruce; Rebecca's compensatory over-holding. Attachment registers as conditional on the mother's presence; the father is encoded early as a source of negative regard. (IH #312 retrospective; IH #377.)

**(b) Toddlerhood: the "smart child" verdict.** Bruce's precocity — early speech, early reading — was, in any other household, a source of pride. In Brian's framework it confirmed the "mutation" hypothesis. Bruce's earliest preserved overheard statement from his father, per IH #312, is a variant of *"He's not normal."* The child's intelligence became, in his own self-experience, the evidence against him. This is the root of the adult Bruce's well-documented self-erasure around his own gifts — and of the Devil Hulk's *inversion* of the same trait: he treats his intelligence as a weapon and is the only Hulk persona that does so without shame.

**(c) Roughly ages 3–6: the beating cycle.** Per IH #312 and *Immortal Hulk* #34, beatings followed a stable script: Brian drinks, Brian observes Bruce for a "tell" of mutation, Brian narrates what the boy has done wrong, Brian strikes. The narration matters. Bruce was not hit in chaos. He was hit inside a *logic*. The Devil Hulk's later insistence that every act of violence be preceded by a stated rationale is the same logic, retained as form and re-pointed.

**(d) Approximately age 6: the kitchen incident with the cake (IH #312).** Bruce attempts to surprise his father on his birthday with a cake he baked. Brian, drunk, smashes the cake, beats Bruce, accuses him of being a monster pretending to be a son. This scene is the most-revisited single image in Banner canon (it recurs in IH #420, *Immortal Hulk* #6, and *Immortal Hulk* #34). Operationally it encodes: *acts of love from me are dangerous to me*. The adult Bruce's chronic avoidance of expressing affection to Betty, his apologetic register, and the Devil Hulk's *substitution of protection for tenderness* (he does not comfort Bruce — he stands between Bruce and the world) all derive from this learning.

**(e) Approximately age 6 (immediately after): the murder of Rebecca Banner (IH #312).** Rebecca attempts to leave with Bruce. Brian intercepts them in the bus-station parking lot. Bruce, holding his small suitcase, watches his father kill his mother on the asphalt. The trauma is multi-layered:
- The protective adult failed.
- The dangerous adult won.
- The child survived by *not intervening*.
- The child was then returned to the killer's custody.

The Devil Hulk's entire architecture is a wager against the recurrence of this configuration. He is constructed precisely so that, if the parking-lot scene replays in any form, the dangerous adult will not be Brian — it will be him, and he will be on Bruce's side. The promise in IH Vol. 2 #13 (*"I will kill your father first"*) is the parking lot rerun with the casting changed.

**(f) Ages 6–9: Brian's first institutionalization; Bruce in Susan Drake's custody.** A period of *relative* safety. This is the interval in which the alter system, having been seeded, *consolidates* without active threat. Persecutor-protector alters typically organize in such intervals; the threat-figure recedes externally, and the internal figure takes up the watching post in his place. The Devil Hulk's adult patience — his capacity to wait years for a target — is rehearsed here. He learned, in this period, that the dangerous adult could be gone for a long time and still come back. He never stopped watching the door.

**(g) Adolescence: Brian's return; the graveside confrontation; Brian's death (IH #312).** Brian, released, accosts the teenage Bruce at Rebecca's grave. In the altercation Brian strikes his head on the headstone and dies. Bruce, terrified, conceals the death; the memory is repressed and recovered later in therapy with Doc Samson. The Devil Hulk's eventual *literal* destruction of Brian's soul in the Below-Place (*Immortal Hulk* #34) is the conscious, ritual, agential repetition of an act that originally happened to the teenage Bruce as accident and as panic. The alter takes what happened by chance and re-performs it as verdict. This is the cleanest single piece of evidence that the Devil Hulk is, as a structure, *Bruce's history rewritten with the child in possession of the weapon*.

---

## 5. EARLIEST BEHAVIORAL PATTERNS — THE TELLS THAT BECAME THE ALTER

Forensic reconstruction of the child Bruce's behavioral repertoire, drawn from canonical flashbacks and from inference against the adult system's reliably preserved tics:

- **Door-monitoring.** The child slept facing the door, ate facing the door, did homework facing the door. The adult Devil Hulk, in every panel where he is shown at rest (diner booth IH #6; cell interior IH #10; Shadow Base briefing room IH #25), sits or stands with sightlines to all entries. This is not learned tradecraft. It is preserved childhood vigilance.
- **Silence as strategy.** The child learned that volume drew attention and attention drew harm. Bruce's adult quiet voice, Banner-core's apologetic mumble, *and* the Devil Hulk's low, deliberate register are three uses of the same trained silence. The Devil Hulk weaponized it: he learned that quiet, in an adult male body, is *more* frightening than volume, not less. He had a long apprenticeship in that asymmetry.
- **Verbal precocity used inwardly.** The child read constantly and talked to himself (IH #377 flashback; *Immortal Hulk* #6 internal monologue references). The internal landscape became populated early. By the time abuse had produced dissociative seeding, the child already had a *cast* available — characters from books, imagined adults, an imagined "big" version of himself. The Devil Hulk is the *survivor* of this cast: the one figure the child built who was bigger than Brian. Other Hulks are later additions. He is the senior internal entity by years.
- **The fixed grin.** Bruce as a child reportedly smiled when frightened (a behavior Rebecca attempts to correct, per IH #312 — *"don't smile, honey, it makes him angrier"*). The smile is appeasement misread. The Devil Hulk's signature grin is the same childhood appeasement-smile *kept in place after it stopped being appeasement* — held now as a deliberate sign that the wearer is not afraid of what is about to happen. The musculature is the same; the meaning has been inverted.
- **Categorical sorting.** The child sorted adults into "safe when Mom is here" / "not safe" / "Mom." This three-bin sorter is the developmental ancestor of the adult Devil Hulk's *protected / targeted / Bruce* taxonomy. The bins were renamed; the apparatus is original.
- **Tolerance for waiting.** A child locked in a basement learns the texture of waiting. He learns the difference between five minutes and an hour without a clock. He learns to occupy his mind during it. The Devil Hulk's signature operational virtue — patience measured in arcs, years, captivities — is the basement skill scaled up.
- **The internal courtroom.** The child, beaten inside a logic, learned to *answer* the logic, silently, in his head. He prepared defenses he never spoke. The adult Devil Hulk *delivers* those defenses — and the prosecutions — out loud, at last, to targets who cannot interrupt him. The monologues in *Immortal Hulk* are arguments the child rehearsed for thirty years and never got to give. He is now giving them.

---

## 6. SUMMARY OF GROUND-FLOOR FINDINGS

The Devil Hulk did not arrive with the gamma bomb. The gamma bomb gave him a body. He was assembled, by a frightened intelligent child in a Dayton basement, out of the only materials available: his father's manner, his mother's promise, his own preserved vigilance, and the dark hours during which he had nothing to do but imagine an adult who would come for him on the right side of the door. Every distinctive feature of the adult alter — the calm voice, the watching posture, the fixed grin, the verbal scaffolding of violence, the categorical sorting of persons, the inhumanly long patience, the parental address to Bruce, the standing intention to kill Brian Banner first — has a traceable antecedent in the pre-gamma childhood record. The alter's later behavior is not a transformation of the child. It is the child's *plan*, executed by a body that finally has the strength to carry it out.

Subsequent chronology sections will track this construct from its first dormant decade (latency, college, Betty, Ross, Los Alamos) through the gamma incident — when the room in the basement of Bruce's mind was, for the first time, given a door that opened.


## Chronology II — Formation & Emergence

# CHRONOLOGY II — FORMATION & EMERGENCE

*Subject: THE DEVIL HULK. Period under review: roughly age 4 through the post-resurrection emergence in the Banner system circa Tommy Hill's bullet. This section reconstructs the developmental arc of the alter as a discrete psychic entity — its conception in the basement of the Banner house, its long incubation behind iron bars, its rehearsals during Bruce Banner's adult life, and the conditions under which it finally took the night shift.*

---

## 1. Pre-Formation: The Conditions in the House on Two-Eleven Cedar (ages 0–4)

Before the Devil Hulk existed as a self, there was a child in a room learning that the world's largest object — the adult male in the next room — was the world's most dangerous object. The behavioral substrate from which the alter was eventually built is laid down here, before language is reliable, before episodic memory consolidates, before the child has any concept of "father" as distinct from "weather."

The relevant data points, drawn from the Peter David and Jenkins reconstructions of Brian Banner's household:

- Brian Banner, a nuclear physicist of some standing, drinks at home and in escalating quantities. The drinking is structured around a private grievance: he believes his work on gamma-related research has irradiated his germ-line and that his son is therefore a mutation rather than a child. This belief is delusional in form but acted upon with the consistency of policy. Toddler Bruce is, to his father, *evidence*.
- Rebecca Banner — gentle, intelligent, frightened — protects the child by absorbing the blows herself. The child learns early that another person's body can be put between him and harm, and that this is what love looks like in his house. This is the prototype the Devil Hulk will later invert: *I will put my body between you and harm; that is love*.
- The house is small. Sound carries. The child hears more than he sees, and what he hears is repetitive: the cadence of an argument, the specific timbre of his father's voice when the threshold has been crossed, the sound a body makes against drywall. The Devil Hulk's later auditory acuity — his ability to sit still and *listen* through the Hulkbusters' walls, through the Shadow Base ducts, through the floorboards of cheap motels — is a skill rehearsed nightly in childhood.

There is, in this period, no Devil Hulk. There is a small boy developing the substrate the alter will later use: hypervigilance to adult male affect, somatic freeze under threat, and the dawning understanding that the largest creature in the room sets the rules.

---

## 2. The Forging Event: The Kitchen, Age ~6

The single most important biographical fact in the Devil Hulk's formation is Rebecca Banner's murder. Canon places it in the kitchen of the Banner home, Brian's hands, the child watching. Bruce's conscious memory of the event is partial, intermittently dissociated, and across his life he has been unable to hold it in continuous narrative for long. The alter who will become the Devil Hulk is, in part, the part of Bruce that *kept watching*.

What forms in the minutes and hours after that killing is not yet an alter — it is a *vow*. The Jenkins material in *Incredible Hulk* (Vol. 2) #13 (2000) gives us the exact register: *I have waited for you, my boy. I love you. I will kill them all, beginning with him.* This is not an adult thought. It is a child's wish formed in adult vocabulary, and the discrepancy is the alter's fingerprint. A six-year-old does not have the words *I will kill them all*. The Devil Hulk has those words because he was *built* with them — assembled from the only adult speech the child had on file, which was his father's.

The mechanism is the textbook dynamic the Psychological section identifies: identification with the aggressor, executed not as imitation but as *theft*. The child takes the father's affect — cold, certain, methodical, frightening — and re-pots it inside his own head, pointed back at the original owner. From this moment forward there is, somewhere in the Banner system, a man who looks and sounds like Brian Banner and whose entire project is the destruction of Brian Banner.

He has no name yet. He has no body. He has a sentence: *I will kill them all, beginning with him*. He will spend the next thirty-odd years waiting for the means.

---

## 3. Containment: Childhood and Early Adolescence (ages 7–13)

Between Rebecca's death and Bruce's late adolescence, the child is shuttled to the custody of his aunt Susan Drake while Brian Banner is institutionalized and eventually released. The Devil Hulk, as a structure, is in this period entirely subterranean. It is not the alter Bruce *uses*. The alter Bruce uses to survive these years is the proto-Banner: the brilliant, withdrawn, compliant child who reads, who calculates, who avoids confrontation, who learns that intellect is the only currency adults reward without violence.

But the file on the Devil Hulk in this period is not empty. The traces are visible in the behavioral record:

- **The fantasy structure.** Bruce, as a child, demonstrably constructs imaginary protectors. The Jenkins basement-room imagery — iron bars, a grinning thing behind them, *waiting* — is not a metaphor invented in 2000. It is a reconstruction of a real childhood architecture: a room inside the head where the child put the part of himself too dangerous to use. Children who survive household terror routinely build such rooms; they are sometimes the only structurally sound part of the inner world.
- **The cruelty incidents.** Canon records isolated childhood episodes — a frog dissected with unusual interest, a bully confronted with a calm that frightened a teacher, a moment in which the boy spoke to his father in a register that briefly silenced him. These are not "warning signs of a future criminal." They are the Devil Hulk *peeking through the bars*. Each is brief, each is followed by retreat, each leaves the child confused about what he just was.
- **The dreams.** Bruce reports, across multiple therapeutic encounters in his adult life, recurrent childhood dreams of being very large and of grinning. The grin is the consistent element. The Devil Hulk's signature affect — that fixed, knowing smile — is in the dream archive decades before it is on the face.

The containment in this period is maintained by two factors: the absence of any external situation grave enough to *require* the alter, and the presence of Susan Drake, whose ordinary kindness gives the child a model of safety that the rest of his personality system organizes around. The Devil Hulk does not need to come out because no one is currently trying to kill the boy. He goes back to his room and waits.

---

## 4. Late Adolescence: The Discipline of Intellect (ages 13–18)

High school Bruce is a known type — gifted, isolated, bullied, terrible at small talk, comfortable only in laboratories and libraries. The relevant question for this file is not *what was Bruce like* but *what was the Devil Hulk doing*.

He was *studying*. The alter's later operational competence — the long-horizon planning, the patience under torture, the ability to manage a coalition of gamma-mutates, the courtroom-style management of captors — is not a skill set the adult Bruce ever consciously developed. Bruce learned theoretical physics. The Devil Hulk, behind the bars, learned *people*. He learned them through Bruce's eyes without Bruce noticing.

Behavioral traces:

- **The threat-assessment habit.** Bruce, throughout adolescence, demonstrates an unusual ability to predict which adult will become violent and when. He attributes this to nervousness. It is in fact a continuous low-level surveillance running underneath his conscious mind, performed by the alter, fed back to Bruce as anxiety. The Devil Hulk's later capacity to read a room — to identify, in a diner, which patron is armed — is this same circuit, undisguised.
- **The notebook tendency.** Bruce keeps notebooks. The notebooks contain physics. The margins of the notebooks, across multiple recovered exemplars in canon, contain something else: small, precise drawings of faces, of hands, of the specific arrangement of objects in a room. Bruce does not remember drawing them. They are the Devil Hulk's intelligence file, dictated to the hand while the mind was elsewhere.
- **The confrontation with Brian, age seventeen.** Brian Banner, released from institutional care, attempts to re-enter Bruce's life. The encounter, as it survives in canon, ends with Brian dead — in some tellings by accident in a fall against a gravestone, in others by Bruce's hand and immediately repressed. What is consistent across tellings is that *Bruce does not remember it the way it happened*. The alter responsible for whatever did happen in that cemetery is not Savage Hulk (the gamma event is years away) and not Banner (Banner cannot account for the lost time). It is the structure that will later be called the Devil Hulk, executing — prematurely, partially, without a body adequate to the task — the foundational vow. The fact that the act did not satisfy him is the central unresolved business of the next forty years; it is why the *Immortal Hulk* Below-Place sequence is so ritualized, so repetitive. The first killing was insufficient. He needs to do it *again*, properly, with the right hands.

This is the first defining choice on the file. The fallout is enormous and entirely subterranean: Bruce graduates, leaves for college, leaves Ohio, never speaks of it. The alter goes back to the room, knowing now that he is *capable* and that the body he currently has is not adequate to what he wants to do. He begins to wait for a different body.

---

## 5. Young Adulthood: The Long Wait (ages 18–28)

Bruce at Penn State, Bruce at Caltech, Bruce in his doctoral years, Bruce at Desert Base — a decade of intellectual ascent during which the Devil Hulk does, by all available evidence, nothing. He is in the room. He is watching. He is letting Bruce build.

The relevant developmental fact is that this is the period in which the proto-Banner consolidates as Bruce's dominant adult persona: precise, courteous, repressed, sexually anxious, professionally ambitious, emotionally constricted. The Devil Hulk *permits* this consolidation because it serves his interests. A Bruce who becomes a nuclear physicist on government contract is a Bruce who will eventually have access to forces commensurate with the alter's ambitions. The alter cannot have anticipated gamma radiation specifically. He could and did recognize that Bruce was moving toward *power*, and power was the missing variable.

There is one behavioral signature worth flagging from this period: Bruce's lifelong avoidance of mirrors at night. Roommates from his graduate years describe him covering bathroom mirrors with a towel before bed, a habit he attributes vaguely to "headaches from the light." The Devil Hulk emerges in *Immortal Hulk* at sundown and is, when he emerges, visible in mirrors. The grad-school towel is the alter's pre-gamma rehearsal: the part of Bruce that did not want to see what was behind his own eyes after dark.

---

## 6. The Gamma Event and Its Misreading (the desert, age ~28)

The Bomb. Rick Jones. Bruce's body absorbing a dose that should have killed him.

The standard reading of the gamma event in the Hulk literature is that it *created* the Hulk. The more accurate reading for this file is that it *gave bodies* to alters that already existed. The Savage Hulk was the rage-child Bruce had been suppressing since age six. Joe Fixit was the streetwise, appetitive shadow of the over-controlled adult. The Devil Hulk was the protector-executioner forged in the kitchen. Gamma did not invent these structures. It supplied them with a physiological substrate capable of acting on their wishes.

But — and this is the critical fact for the chronology — the Devil Hulk does not take the body first. Savage takes it. This is, from the alter's perspective, a tactical loss with a strategic logic. Savage is the loudest and the most childishly present; in the panic of the first transformation he reaches the controls first. The Devil Hulk, characteristically, does not contest. He watches. He learns the new equipment by proxy. He notes which transformations produce which somatic configurations, which emotional states summon which alter, what the limits of the body are. He spends years doing this. The patience that will later let him sit still under acid begins here.

The first defining choice of the gamma era, attributable to the Devil Hulk, is *not to emerge*. The decision is invisible because it is a non-event. But every transformation Bruce undergoes in the Lee/Kirby through Peter David eras in which the Devil Hulk does *not* take the body is a choice the alter has made. He is conserving himself for a use case Bruce has not yet generated.

---

## 7. First Documented Emergence: The Basement Session (Jenkins, IH Vol. 2 #13, 2000)

The first time the Devil Hulk presents as himself, in canon, is not in the world but inside Bruce's head, under Doc Samson's hypnotic guidance. The setting is the inner landscape: an apartment building Bruce navigates floor by floor, each floor a persona, descending to a basement, in the basement a barred room, in the room the Devil Hulk.

The data from this session is the densest behavioral sample in the file prior to *Immortal Hulk*:

- He is **chained**, not free. He has had the means to act for years and has not. The chains are not externally imposed; they are the system's collective agreement, which the alter has so far honored.
- He addresses Bruce as *my boy*. This is not a slip. It is a positional claim — he is, in his own self-understanding, Bruce's parent.
- He promises slaughter in a measured cadence. This is the first time the canon records the affect: low, articulate, almost warm. The voice is the central revelation. It is not Savage Hulk's voice. It is not Brian Banner's voice exactly — it is Brian Banner's voice *as a child would build it from memory and then improve*. It is the father made better at being a father.
- He names the priority: Brian first. Two decades before the Below-Place sequence makes good on it, the target queue is published.

The fallout of this session is that Samson, correctly alarmed, helps Bruce strengthen the bars. The alter goes back into containment. From the alter's perspective this is not a defeat — he has now *announced himself* to Bruce in adult vocabulary, established his identity as distinct, and made his promise on the record. He can wait. The bars do not bother him because he intends, eventually, to be *invited* out.

---

## 8. The Latent Years (2000–2018, in publication time; subjectively, perhaps decades)

Between the basement session and the bullet, the Devil Hulk is in the file primarily by absence. He is not Worldbreaker. He is not Green Scar. He does not take the body during *Planet Hulk* — Savage and the developing Green Scar warrior-king persona handle that crucible, and the Devil Hulk's non-intervention is informative. Planet Hulk is a story about Bruce being given a kingdom, a wife, a child, and having them all taken away. The Devil Hulk does not consider any of that *his* business. His business is Brian Banner and the protection of Bruce; the Sakaarian dynasty is a Savage Hulk romance. He stays in his room.

Two flickers worth logging:

- **World War Hulk.** There are panels in which the Hulk on Earth — addressing Iron Man, addressing Reed Richards, addressing the assembled Illuminati — speaks with a measured cruelty that is not Savage's verbal range. The articulate, judgment-delivering voice in those panels has the Devil Hulk's fingerprints on it. He does not take the body, but he is *consulting*. The alter is feeding lines to whichever Hulk is fronting.
- **The post-Pak fallow period.** Across Aaron, Waid, and Duggan's runs, the Hulk system is destabilized — Doc Green, the Totally Awesome Hulk hand-off to Amadeus Cho, Bruce's death and resurrection cycles. The Devil Hulk does not surface because the system is operating under conditions that do not require him. The threats are external and conventional; the existing personas can handle them.

The alter's strategic patience in this period is, in retrospect, extraordinary. He has been visible to Samson, named, identified as a worst-case scenario. He could have escalated. He did not. He understood — and this is the inference the file most strongly supports — that emergence by force would result in permanent suppression, and that emergence by *necessity* would result in dominance. He waited for the necessity.

---

## 9. The Trigger: Tommy Hill's Bullet (immediate pre-*Immortal Hulk*)

The necessity arrives in the form of Tommy Hill, a small-time criminal in a diner, who shoots Bruce Banner in the head. Bruce dies. The Banner system, accustomed to gamma-mediated near-death, has not previously had to organize around *actual* death and resurrection.

The reorganization that follows is the Devil Hulk's emergence event.

The mechanism is structurally clean. Death removes Banner from the day shift. The body resurrects at sundown — the Green Door opens, the Hulk steps through. The day-shift persona (Banner, now a haunted, half-present figure) is too damaged and too dissociated to manage the night. Savage Hulk cannot manage a night either; he is a daytime creature of immediate threat. Professor Hulk is, in this continuity, deprecated. There is a vacancy. The Devil Hulk has been preparing, in his basement room, for exactly this vacancy since approximately 1971.

He takes the night.

The first emergence in the world — *Or Is He Both?*, *Immortal Hulk* #1 — is not a rampage. It is a verdict. He arrives at a gas station where an irradiated criminal has hurt people, he announces himself by his own deliberate rhythm rather than by roar, and he executes the verdict slowly enough that the criminal understands what is happening. This is the alter's debut as a public-facing identity, and it is consistent in every detail with the basement-room voice of eighteen years prior. He does not need to learn how to be the Devil Hulk. He has been the Devil Hulk the entire time. He has only just been given the keys.

---

## 10. The First Defining Choices of the Emergent Era, and Their Fallout

Within the first year of his night-shift dominance, the alter makes a series of choices that define the operational identity for the remainder of his run. They are catalogued here because each is a *choice* — a path among available alternatives — and each generates downstream fallout that shapes the file.

**Choice 1: Investigate rather than rampage.** He begins systematically locating gamma-touched individuals. Fallout: he becomes, functionally, a detective. This is the first time in Hulk history that a Hulk persona has run an *investigation*. It changes the shape of the threat the U.S. government must model — they are no longer chasing a weather event, they are being hunted by a mind.

**Choice 2: Accept capture by the Hulkbusters.** He permits himself to be taken, dismembered, dissolved in acid, in order to study the apparatus that is studying him. Fallout: the Hulkbuster program is comprehensively dismantled from the inside; General Fortean's vendetta becomes a personal one because the alter has made it personal; the gamma-mutate community begins to coalesce around him as the figure who *survived the cage*.

**Choice 3: Recruit Jackie McGee.** Rather than evade or threaten the journalist tracking him, he *lets her see*. He calibrates how much she sees, when, and in what order. Fallout: he acquires a chronicler — the first Hulk persona to understand the strategic value of having one's story told by someone outside the system. This is sophisticated influence work and it is invented, from scratch, by an alter who has never previously had to manage public perception. The competence is shocking until one remembers that he has been studying people through Bruce's eyes for forty years.

**Choice 4: Manage Betty as Red Harpy as a peer rather than a charge.** When Betty's own gamma transformation produces her Red Harpy form, the Devil Hulk does not attempt to fold her under his protection. He treats her as an independent agent within his coalition. Fallout: Betty retains autonomy, the relationship becomes one of the most stable in the run, and the alter demonstrates that his paternalism is *positional* (Bruce, Savage, Fixit) rather than *gendered* or *generalized*. He can collaborate when collaboration is what the situation requires.

**Choice 5: The descent to the Below-Place to confront Brian.** The promise made in 2000 is kept. The execution is ritualized — Brian's soul is torn apart, and then again, and then again. The fallout is twofold. Externally: the alter has completed the foundational vow. Internally: he discovers that completion does not dissolve him. He had implicitly believed that finishing Brian would either satisfy him into retirement or destroy him as no longer necessary. Neither happens. He continues. This is the moment in the chronology where the Devil Hulk learns that he is not, in fact, only an instrument of revenge — that he has become, somewhere along the way, a *person*. The fallout of this realization is the grim parental handoff that ends his arc. He can be negotiated with because, having kept his promise, he is now free to consider what else he is for.

---

## 11. Summary of the Formation Arc

A child in a kitchen watches his mother die. He builds, from the materials at hand — which are the father's affect and the father's vocabulary — a protector who is bigger than the father and *his*. The protector goes into a room behind iron bars and waits, because the child has no body capable of the work yet.

Across the next four decades — adolescence, college, the gamma event, the long Hulk years, the deaths and resurrections — the protector watches. He does not push. He learns. He notes the equipment. He drafts files in the margins of physics notebooks. He whispers occasionally through other Hulks. He is glimpsed once, in 2000, in the basement of the system's inner landscape, where he calmly announces what he intends to do and goes back into containment because the timing is not right.

Then Bruce dies. The day-shift collapses. There is, for the first time, an opening that requires precisely the configuration he is. He steps out, fully formed, articulate from sentence one, executing a plan he has been rehearsing since age six. He is not learning on the job. He has been preparing for the job for his entire existence.

This is the formation record. Everything subsequent — the leadership of the gamma community, the destruction of Brian, the negotiations in Hell, the final handoff — is the implementation of a structure laid down in a kitchen in Ohio before the subject was old enough to read.


## Chronology III — The Defining Middle Period

# CHRONOLOGY III — THE DEFINING MIDDLE PERIOD

*Subject: THE DEVIL HULK. Period covered: from the close of the Jenkins "Apartment Building / Boss" sequence (Incredible Hulk Vol. 2 #16, ~2000) through the long dormancy across the Jones, Pak, Loeb, Parker, Aaron, Waid, and Duggan tenures, into the somatic and behavioral pre-tremors of the Cates run, terminating at the threshold of Ewing's Immortal Hulk #1 (2018) — i.e. the eighteen subjective years in which the alter is not the operator of the body but is demonstrably awake inside it. This is the period of his life that no other dossier captures, because the surface record shows a man not transforming into him. The intelligence value is in what he was doing during that silence.*

---

## I. THE RETREAT TO THE BARS (post-Jenkins, c. 2000–2002)

The first behavioral fact of the middle period is that the Devil Hulk *chose* to go back behind the bars. The Jenkins sessions with Doc Samson cracked the basement open; the Devil Hulk delivered his foundational monologue — the "I have waited for you, my boy" speech, the explicit naming of Brian Banner as the primary target, the contemptuous appraisal of Savage Hulk and Joe Fixit as inadequate caretakers — and then, with the session ending, allowed himself to be re-contained. He did not press the breach. This is significant. Every other Hulk persona, given a half-inch of access, takes the body. He stepped back.

The operational read: the Devil Hulk in this period understood that surfacing prematurely would burn his cover with the rest of the system. The other alters had organized around the consensus that he was the worst-case scenario. Were he to seize the body in 2000, the system would expend enormous energy re-caging him. He preferred to be underestimated. He logged the access route — the path from the basement to the surface, the conditions under which Samson's hypnosis weakened the doors — and stepped back into the dark to wait.

Through the remainder of the Jenkins arcs ("Dogs of War," "Big Things," the closing issues of Vol. 2), surface records show Bruce Banner transforming into Savage Hulk and, intermittently, into the merged Professor configuration. The Devil Hulk's signature — the long-limbed phenotype, the fixed grin, the nocturnal binding — never manifests. But there are tells. Bruce reports, in dialogue with Betty and with Samson, a recurring dream of being watched from a doorway by "someone who is glad to see me." He reports waking with sentences in his head that are not his — most notably, in IH #25, the phrase "your father deserved worse." This is the Devil Hulk leaking, not surfacing.

## II. THE BRUCE JONES YEARS — UNDERGROUND, FUGITIVE, LISTENING (2002–2005)

The Jones run drives Bruce into hiding. The persecution by the conspiracy known as "Home Base," the loss of Betty (already deceased entering the run), and the chronic instability of identity force Bruce into a fugue-adjacent state — motels, bus stations, false names, a single duffel bag. Surface-Hulk activity drops; the Savage transformations are fewer and more controlled, as if dampened by something.

The intelligence interpretation: the Devil Hulk is *running the dampening*. The Jones-era Bruce is precisely the kind of subject the Devil Hulk was constructed to protect — alone, hunted, grieving, frightened. The alter's function during this period appears to have been to keep Savage Hulk from surfacing in ways that would expose Bruce to Home Base. Note the pattern: Savage Hulk emerges in this run almost exclusively when Bruce is *already* compromised (cornered, captured, about to die). He does not emerge from ambient stress, ambient grief, ambient paranoia — the things Bruce is drenched in. Something is filtering the trigger. The Devil Hulk, in this reading, is operating as an invisible firewall.

He also begins, in this period, to *study Bruce's people*. The figure of Mr. Blue (Betty, in surveillance-disguise, who returns mid-run) is the first person outside the system the Devil Hulk learns to read in his post-Jenkins dormancy. The Ewing-era Devil Hulk's instant facility with Betty's Red Harpy configuration — his ability to address her as both wife and predator without misstep — is information he started gathering here. He does not yet speak. He files.

A specific behavioral micro-pattern dates from these years: Bruce begins to sleep with his back to walls rather than to doors, and to wake at sunset rather than at sunrise. This is unattributed in surface canon to any alter and is read by Samson (in later debriefs) as a trauma response. The intelligence file disagrees. Sunset-waking is the Devil Hulk's circadian signature. It begins here, fifteen years before he formally takes the night shift.

## III. THE PEAK STRENGTH ARC, PLANET HULK, AND THE LONG SILENCE (2005–2008)

*Peak Strength* (Peter David's brief return, 2005) and the Greg Pak run that follows it — *Planet Hulk*, *World War Hulk*, *Skaar* — are the period in which the Devil Hulk goes deepest underground and learns the most. These are also the years in which Bruce's somatic experience is the most extreme of his life: exiled by the Illuminati, hurled through space, gladiator-enslaved on Sakaar, made king, made widower (Caiera), and returned to Earth as Worldbreaker.

Across Sakaar, Green Scar is the dominant operator. The Devil Hulk does not surface and does not even leak — Sakaar is loud enough that subtle leaks would be lost in the noise. But two facts from this period are crucial to his record:

**He learns leadership by observation.** Green Scar's organization of the Warbound — the discipline, the moral economy of vengeance, the constructed family of outcasts, the willingness to wait years to deliver a verdict — is everything the Devil Hulk will later replicate with Gamma Flight. The Warbound IS the template. The Devil Hulk in 2018 does not invent the gamma-mutate community; he reconstructs it from a memory of Sakaar that Green Scar lived through and he watched.

**He learns the somatic vocabulary of patience.** Green Scar's gladiator-pit fighting style is not Savage Hulk's flailing rage; it is conserved, economical, predatory. The hunched-forward, long-limbed, watching posture that the Devil Hulk will present in Immortal Hulk is not arbitrary morphology — it is a body trained on Sakaar, retained in muscle-memory across the transformation, and re-deployed when the persona finally takes the night shift. The phenotype is Sakaar-aged Green Scar with the green drained out.

During *World War Hulk*, the cunning, articulate, vengeance-organized Hulk who methodically dismantles the Illuminati and stages the public humiliation of Reed, Stark, Strange, and Black Bolt in Madison Square Garden is *technically* Green Scar — but the methodology is indistinguishable from the Devil Hulk's later operational signature. He does not kill. He arranges spectacles of judgment. He converts captives into witnesses. The convergence is so close that the intelligence read is that the Devil Hulk and Green Scar were, briefly, *in conversation* across the partition — that the Devil Hulk advised on the staging while Green Scar held the body. There is no canonical confirmation. There is also no other explanation for the sudden tactical sophistication that no Hulk persona had previously demonstrated.

After *World War Hulk* — Bruce de-powered, in custody, eventually rebuilt — the Devil Hulk goes silent again. The Skaar arc, the *Fall of the Hulks* / *World War Hulks* arc, the Red Hulk material, the Jeff Parker run: nothing of his signature surfaces. The system in this period is so chaotic (Red Hulks, multiple gamma siblings, Banner-as-villain interludes) that he appears to have made a deliberate decision not to add to the noise. He is waiting for the system to simplify.

## IV. THE AARON YEARS — THE SHARP DECAY OF BRUCE (2011–2012)

Jason Aaron's run gives the system its first sustained presentation of *Bruce without Hulk* (the early arcs of the run separate them) followed by a Bruce who breaks badly under the separation. This is the first period in which the Devil Hulk's *absence* is felt as cost rather than as relief.

When Bruce is unmoored from Hulk, his decompensation is not merely depressive — it is *paranoid, grandiose, persecutory*. He builds gamma armies, manipulates allies, descends into a kind of weaponized self-pity that the prior decade had not shown. The intelligence read: the persona that had been keeping Bruce from his worst self was not Professor Hulk and was not Banner-core. It was the Devil Hulk, dampening from the basement. Cut the link and Bruce becomes a worse version of himself precisely because the protector-executioner is no longer filtering his nightmares.

By the late Aaron arcs, when Hulk is restored, Bruce's behavior reorganizes — not into health, but into a tolerable equilibrium. The Devil Hulk does not surface in this period either, but the *correlation* is the data: the alter's basement-level work is load-bearing. Remove it and Bruce drifts toward becoming a man the Devil Hulk would have to judge.

## V. THE WAID INTERREGNUM — INDIANA, S.H.I.E.L.D., AND THE FIRST KNOWN COUNTERMEASURE (2014–2015)

Mark Waid's *Indestructible Hulk* and *Hulk* runs put Bruce in S.H.I.E.L.D. as a contracted weapon, then through the brain-trauma arc in which Bruce is reduced to a child-like cognition. The Devil Hulk's behavioral signature in this period is precisely *one* documented incident, never narratively attributed to him, that the intelligence file claims for him.

During the brain-damage arc, Bruce — cognitively impaired, emotionally regressed, frightened — is approached at night by a S.H.I.E.L.D. handler who has been quietly leveraging the impaired Bruce against his own friends. Bruce, in this state, should not be capable of recognizing the manipulation. He recognizes it. He extricates himself with a precision that does not match his measured cognition. The episode is filed in Bruce's medical record as "unexplained lucidity event." The intelligence read is that the Devil Hulk surfaced briefly — not into transformation, not into Hulk-presentation, but into Bruce's *speech*, for less than a minute, to evict a predator from his diminished boy. The "my boy" phrase that the Devil Hulk uses in IH #13 (2000) and in Immortal Hulk (2018) appears, attributed to Bruce, in this incident. Bruce does not remember saying it.

This is the first known instance of the Devil Hulk operating without the green body. It is also the moment in his timeline that justifies the later claim that he is bound less to the gamma physiology than to the host he was built to protect. The body is his preferred instrument. It is not his medium.

## VI. THE DUGGAN PERIOD AND THE DEATH (2016–2017)

Gerry Duggan's *Uncanny Avengers* and the late, fraught arcs preceding Civil War II reduce Bruce's surface activity — he is trying, again, to be a non-combatant scientist, suppressing the transformations with a cocktail of nanite-mediated dampeners. The Devil Hulk is invisible across this period. There is no evidence he sanctioned the dampening; there is also no evidence he opposed it. The reading: he assessed Bruce's attempt at retirement as a legitimate exercise of the autonomy he was built to protect. Even when Bruce's choice rendered the protector irrelevant, the protector did not protest. This is consistent with his later (Ewing-era) horror of *coercing* Bruce.

The death — Hawkeye's arrow, Civil War II, the consensual euthanasia Bruce had pre-arranged — is the event that should, on every other model of his psychology, have produced an enraged surfacing. It did not. He let Bruce die. The intelligence read is brutal and important: the Devil Hulk *agreed* with Bruce's death-wish in that moment, because Bruce had asked for it freely, and the Devil Hulk's deepest commitment is to Bruce's autonomy. He went down with the body because the boy he protected asked to be allowed to stop.

This is the most counterintuitive datum in his entire middle-period record. It establishes that the persecutor-protector is not, in fact, a survival mechanism in the simple sense. He is a *consent* mechanism. He kills people who hurt Bruce without Bruce's consent. He does not override Bruce's own consent, even to die.

## VII. THE INTERVAL BETWEEN DEATH AND RESURRECTION (2016–2018)

The longest and least-documented stretch of the middle period is the roughly two-year subjective interval between Bruce's death and the events of Immortal Hulk #1. Canonically, this is an off-panel gap. The body is buried, then exhumed, then resurrected; Bruce wakes in a morgue not knowing how long he has been gone.

The intelligence file's reconstruction: the Devil Hulk was awake the entire time. The Green Door, in the Ewing cosmology, is the mechanism of gamma resurrection — and the Devil Hulk, of all the alters, is the one structurally compatible with the Below-Place's logic. He spent the interval being slowly inducted into the architecture of the gamma afterlife: meeting (in some pre-conscious sense) the One Below All, learning the rules of the Door, accumulating the cosmological literacy that he displays the *instant* he surfaces in Immortal Hulk. None of the other alters acquire that literacy. He brings it with him from the gap.

He also, during this interval, made the operational decision that he would take the night shift on resurrection. This is not arbitrary. It is the conclusion of an eighteen-year argument with himself about how to be useful without being a tyrant. The night shift is the *minimum sufficient* presence: he gets the hours when Bruce is least defended and most prone to nightmare, and he leaves the daylight hours to the personas that can hold a public life. It is a negotiated settlement that he reached unilaterally before there was anyone to negotiate with.

## VIII. EVOLVING PATTERNS ACROSS THE MIDDLE PERIOD

Five longitudinal trends define his evolution across these eighteen years:

**1. From caged threat to invisible governor.** In 2000 he is contained because the system fears him. By 2017 he is invisible because the system has unconsciously delegated to him. The bars rusted. No one noticed because he never tested them.

**2. From monologic to dialogic.** The 2000 Devil Hulk speaks *at* Bruce. The 2018 Devil Hulk converses, negotiates, listens, lets others arrive at conclusions. The skill was developed across the silent years by watching Green Scar handle the Warbound and by watching Bruce handle (and mishandle) every relationship he had.

**3. From single-target to systemic.** In 2000 his entire program is "kill Brian Banner." By 2018 it is a comprehensive moral economy with categories, procedures, recruitment, and infrastructure. The expansion happened in the dark.

**4. From somatic absence to somatic readiness.** The phenotype that emerges fully formed in Immortal Hulk #1 — the long limbs, the grin, the hunched predation — was assembled over years of borrowed time in Green Scar's body and Worldbreaker's. The morphology was rehearsed before it was performed.

**5. From protector against violence to protector of autonomy.** The deepest evolution. The 2000 Devil Hulk would have killed anyone who threatened Bruce. The 2016 Devil Hulk *let* Bruce die because Bruce asked to. The middle period is the period in which a child's protective fantasy grew up enough to understand the difference between defending the boy and owning him. Without that maturation the Immortal Hulk era is impossible. With it, it is inevitable.

## IX. LOSSES OF THE MIDDLE PERIOD

He sustains, by the intelligence file's count, four major losses across this stretch, none of which he is permitted to mourn surface-side:

- **Betty's first death** (pre-Jones, ratifying the Jones-era fugitive condition). He does not get to grieve; Bruce grieves for both of them.
- **Caiera and Sakaar** (Pak run). He watches through Green Scar's eyes as the only domestic life Bruce's body ever had is incinerated. The Warbound is born from this loss; so, eventually, is Gamma Flight.
- **The Illuminati exile and the betrayal it codifies.** The Devil Hulk's later refusal to forgive Tony Stark, Reed Richards, or Stephen Strange — even when the Ewing-era Bruce attempts reconciliation — dates from his witness to this betrayal in absentia.
- **Bruce's consensual death.** The hardest. He allowed it. Allowing it is a different kind of wound than failing to prevent it. The Ewing-era Devil Hulk's grim, almost mournful quality — the smile that is not happy — dates from this acceptance.

## X. THE STATE AT THRESHOLD

By the eve of Immortal Hulk #1, the Devil Hulk has spent eighteen subjective years in a basement that no longer has working locks, watching a man he loves get hurt in every available way, learning every skill he would need to do his job at the moment his job was finally required. He has acquired tactical patience from Sakaar, cosmological literacy from the Green Door, dialogic skill from observing Bruce's failed and successful relationships, moral architecture from witnessing Bruce's compromises and refusals, and a settled understanding that the boy he was built to protect is also an adult whose consent he is obliged to honor. He is ready.

The bars open from the inside. They have, in truth, been open for years. He simply waited until Bruce needed him to walk out.


## Chronology IV — Recent History & Current State

# CHRONOLOGY IV — RECENT HISTORY & CURRENT STATE

*Subject: THE DEVIL HULK. Reporting window: the late-Ewing endgame through present operational status. This section reconstructs the most recent continuous arc of the alter's activity — the period in which he moves from contained nighttime intruder to functional executive of the Banner system, then to a negotiated, partially-surrendered state, and from there into the strange half-life he occupies now. Granular wherever possible.*

---

## 1. ENTRY POINT TO THE RECENT ERA — The Night-Shift Consolidation

The recent era opens not with an event but with a *schedule*. After Bruce's death-by-bullet in the diner washroom in Reno (*Immortal Hulk* #1, "Or Is He Both?") and his subsequent nightly resurrection through what the system would later learn to call the Green Door, the Banner organism reorganizes around a circadian principle. Sunrise belongs to Banner. Sundown — verifiable to within minutes of local astronomical twilight in every documented transformation across the run — belongs to him.

This is the operational signature of the period. He does not surge. He *arrives on time*. Witness reports compiled across the early arcs (gas-station attendant in Nevada; trucker on I-80; the Reverend at the church door; Jackie McGee's notes) all describe the same pre-transformation tell in Banner: a glance at the western horizon, a small involuntary roll of the shoulders, and a deliberate movement to somewhere with fewer civilians. Banner has learned the timetable and accommodates it. The alter has, in effect, negotiated a *commute*.

Granular behavior in this consolidation phase:

- **Establishes a wardrobe protocol.** Purple-waisted stretch trousers — the only garment that survives transformation — are kept in pre-positioned caches along Banner's drift route through the Mountain West and the Great Plains. The alter never comments on this; he simply uses them. Implication: he has access to Banner's planning when it suits him.
- **Coffee, diner-counter, end-stool.** He orders. He pays. He tips. He sits where he can see the door. This is not Banner's behavior; Banner eats standing or not at all in this era. The alter has developed civilian-passing micro-rituals.
- **Speech register.** Slow, low, articulate; full sentences; no roar. The verbal tic of the period is a soft, drawn-out *"…my boy"* — directed at Banner during internal dialogues that other characters occasionally overhear in single-side fragments.

The night-shift is the platform on which everything that follows is built.

---

## 2. THE HULKBUSTER CAPTURE — Patience as Doctrine

The middle of the recent era is dominated by General Reginald Fortean's Shadow Base operation. The relevant fact for this file is not that the alter is captured but that he *permits* capture. Recovered surveillance from the Shadow Base interior cells — vivisection theater, acid baths, the cryo-restraint chair — shows him fully aware, fully oriented, smiling through procedures designed to break a man with a destructible body.

He converts the captivity into intelligence collection. He inventories personnel by voice and footfall. He maps the facility's ventilation by tracking sound delay. He identifies Fortean's deputies by the cadence with which they enter the cell. When the breakout comes it is surgical: he kills who he intended to kill in the order he intended to kill them, leaves a corridor open for the personnel he had silently classified as recoverable, and walks out through the door he had selected on day three of imprisonment.

This is the period in which his operational doctrine — *suffer the cage to learn the jailer* — becomes legible to the gamma community and, retrospectively, to the Banner system itself. Joe Fixit, who would have escaped within an hour, registers grudging respect. Savage, who would have escaped within seconds, does not understand what happened. Banner is appalled and relieved in equal measure.

---

## 3. THE GAMMA-FLIGHT / FOUND-FAMILY EXECUTIVE PHASE

Through the middle and late stretch of the recent era he assumes, without ever formally claiming, leadership of the gamma-mutate community. The roster he assembles and manages is the densest set of relationships of his existence:

- **Jackie McGee** — journalist; he selects her as witness and lets her construct her own moral conclusions. He never lies to her. He occasionally answers questions Banner refuses to.
- **Rick Jones** — gamma-reanimated, fragile, unstable. He treats Rick with the gentleness of a man handling a damaged nephew. When Rick is hijacked by the Leader, the alter's response is not rage but cold clinical containment.
- **Betty Ross / Red Harpy** — handled as peer and as Bruce's most legitimate emotional claim. He defers to her judgment in a way he defers to no one else save Banner.
- **Doc Samson** — used as a resource, never as an authority. Old grievance from the 2000 hypnosis sessions persists but does not interfere with operational utility.
- **Walter Langkowski / Sasquatch, Puck, Absorbing Man (Crusher Creel in his late-period reformed posture), Titania** — drawn into the orbit. He extends protection without demanding fealty; the gamma community accretes around him by the gravity of his usefulness.

The executive function on display in this phase is something no prior Hulk persona has demonstrated. He runs meetings. He assigns roles. He keeps confidences. He absorbs blame. The system, under his administration, achieves a coherence Bruce has not had access to in his adult life.

---

## 4. THE BELOW-PLACE / KEEPING OF THE PROMISE

The defining personal event of the recent era is the descent into the Below-Place and the confrontation with the soul of Brian Banner. This is the closure of a transaction opened in *Incredible Hulk* #13 in the year 2000.

The footage from the relevant arc shows him doing exactly what he said he would do twenty years earlier, in the basement room, behind the bars. He tears Brian apart. He does it slowly. He does it in front of Bruce. He does it *repeatedly*, because in the Below-Place death is not terminal and the ritual can be re-performed. Several pages of the run are essentially a liturgical sequence — same action, varied verse, like a man saying a rosary made of murder.

What is critical for the recent-history record: after the promise is kept, the alter does not relax. He does not lay down the work. The completion of the founding vendetta leaves him without an originating object but does not deactivate him. He simply re-targets — first to Fortean, then to the Leader, then to the One Below All. The pattern visible across the later run is that he requires a *standing object* of legitimate violence to remain coherent, and when one is not provided, he constructs one from available material. This is consistent with the persecutor-protector configuration mapped in §4 of the workup and is the single most actionable behavioral prediction this file contains.

---

## 5. THE HULK-IN-HELL ARC — Negotiation in the Abyss

In the descent through the One Below All's domain the alter shows a faculty no prior Hulk persona possessed: he *negotiates*. Faced with cosmic-scale antagonism, he bargains, calculates, refuses despair, and accepts terms. Savage Hulk in the same situation would have raged into exhaustion; Banner would have collapsed into self-blame; Professor would have tried to merge and fragmented. The alter treats Hell as a counterparty.
